## Key findings from Round 1:

### Adversary discovered:
1. **Strong vs Weak ZSF dichotomy**: 
   - Strongly ZSF (no zero-sum at weight ≤ p): ONLY the doubled+e1 construction
   - Weakly ZSF (no zero-sum at weight ≤ p-1, HAS one at weight p): ALL other ZSF sets
   
2. **Shadow saturation is FALSE for general ZSF sets** - cap-based ZSF at p=7 covers only 2244/2400


3. **Shadow saturation is TRUE for strongly ZSF sets** - the doubled+e1 construction exhibits this property consistently across p=5,7,11,13,17,19,23 and also at r=4, p=5.
4. **No 18-vector strongly ZSF set exists** - exhaustive search confirms this at p=5,7,11.

### Proof strategy from Adversary:
The approach hinges on showing that any 17-vector subset either contains a weight-≤(p-1) zero-sum (which immediately yields GRAIL), or if it's itself ZSF, then distinguishing whether it's weakly or strongly ZSF determines the next step.

(B) Shadow saturation for doubled+e1 at all p

The crucial constraint is that we can use at most one copy of each vector, which means each coefficient is bounded by p-1, and this gives us a zero-sum at a specific weight equal to the sum of those coefficients. subsequence can choose MULTIPLE copies of the same vector (different coefficients c_1, c_2 on the same v), contributing (c_1+c_2)·v

So: a zero-sum subsequence of the pool gives a zero-sum representation BUT the coefficient on each vector could be any sum of distinct elements from {1,...,p-1}, not just a single element of {0,...,p-1}.

In our shadow language, we restrict c_v ∈ {0,...,p-1} (a single coefficient). The copies-pool language allows "multi-coefficient" usage.

This abundance of zero-sums means many lifts to F_p^{r+1} exist, each with weight at most p, which creates the contradiction I need to rule out this configuration.

Now for fiber w = c ≠ 0: the constraint becomes tighter. A zero-sum in F_p^{r+1} requires the first coordinate to satisfy c·Σc_i ≡ 0 mod p, forcing Σc_i = p exactly. This means I need a weight-p zero-sum specifically. The s-bound gives s(C_p^r) = α(r)(p-1)+p, and since the tail sequence has length (2α(r)+1)(p-1), the inequality 2α(r)+1 ≥ α(r)+2 ensures there's enough room for such a zero-sum to exist.

So the one-fiber case is ruled out for strongly ZSF. If the maximum fiber has size α(r)+1, the remaining α(r) vectors distribute across other fibers—either all in one fiber giving the doubled+e1 structure, or split further.

When vectors spread across three or more fibers, I need to check if cross-fiber triples force zero-sums. Three vectors from different fibers would trigger a rank-3 constraint if they're collinear with a non-degenerate dependency, but in higher dimensions that's a codimension-2 condition, so collinearity isn't guaranteed. This means I can't immediately conclude that strongly ZSF forces at most two fibers.

Let me consolidate what I can actually establish: the zero-fiber has size at most α(r) since η fires on tails, no single fiber can contain all 2α(r)+1 vectors without contradicting either η or the constraint s, and if the distribution splits as (α(r), α(r)+1) then the tails must form a maximal ZSF in F_p^r. These feel like solid partial results worth keeping.

Actually, let me push harder on the r=3 case specifically—with α(3)=8 and total size 17 in F_p^4, maybe I can force the two-fiber structure there.

Now I'm testing whether a (9, 7, 1) distribution with three fibers could work. The single vector in the third fiber is the key constraint: if it's collinear with any pair from the other two fibers, a non-degenerate triple forms and η fires. With 63 possible pairs from the 9×7 combinations, there are many lines in F_p^4 that could contain this singleton vector, so it seems hard to avoid the constraint entirely. -1 affine points in each fiber other than c and d).

For p = 5: 63 lines, each with 4 affine points in fiber e. Max 252 points on lines. Total in fiber e: 124 (nonzero, non-proportional). But 252 > 124 (with overlaps). So every point in fiber e is on some line! Cross-fiber collinear triple exists. Rank-3 fires (assuming non-degenerate). Contradicts strongly ZSF!

This would suggest the (9, 7, 1) distribution is impossible at p = 5, but I need to account for the fact that these 63 lines have significant overlaps, and which affine points actually lie on them depends heavily on the specific choice of vectors. The non-degeneracy conditions also vary depending on which fibers I'm examining—some dependencies might be degenerate for particular fiber triples, which could affect whether rank-3 actually fires.

This line of reasoning is getting quite intricate, so I'll set it aside as a potential avenue to explore further. Let me turn to the (9, 8) distribution instead, where fiber 0 has size 8. 0 mod p for the zero-sum condition, but the Davenport constant s(C_p^3) = 9p-8 is larger than the tail sequence length of 9p-9, so s doesn't fire and we don't get a forced weight-p zero-sum from the 9 nonzero tails in fiber 1.

This is consistent with doubled+e1, which has 8 nonzero tails and 1 zero tail in fiber 1 with no weight-p zero-sum. But if all 9 tails were nonzero instead, we'd have 9(p-1) elements—just one short of s—still with no guaranteed weight-p zero-sum. The question becomes whether a strongly ZSF set could actually have 9 nonzero tails in some fiber c ≠ 0, since the η-fires at weight ≤ p might not correspond to zero-sums if the coordinate sum isn't ≡ 0 mod p.

Only weight p lifts to zero-sums when gcd(k, p) = 1, and s doesn't fire, so 9 nonzero tails in a nonzero fiber might work for strongly ZSF. But doubled+e1 has that one zero tail, so I'm wondering whether all-nonzero tails is actually achievable. The non-proportionality condition matters here—in doubled+e1, fiber 1 has the 8 vectors (1, a) for a ∈ A_8 plus (1, 0), giving 9 distinct projective points. If I replaced (1, 0) with (1, a_9) for some a_9 ∉ A_8, I'd get 9 nonzero tails, and the adversary tested whether this configuration would still be strongly ZSF.

The adversary's testing at p=7 revealed something crucial: all the swap alternatives are only weakly ZSF, while doubled+e1 with its zero tail is the only strongly ZSF configuration. So that zero tail in fiber 1 is actually essential—it's a structural requirement for strong ZSF. The zero tail (vector c·e_1) enables a specific type of zero-sum constraint where the coefficient on e_1 contributes to the first coordinate but not to the tails, and the remaining 8 vectors with their tails in A_8 can form zero-sums under certain weight and modular conditions.

Now I'm checking what happens without that zero tail: all 9 tails become nonzero, which forces any zero-sum within a fiber to satisfy Σc_i ≡ 0 mod p with Σc_i ≤ p, meaning Σc_i = p.

The adversary's observation is that for any choice of 9 nonzero vectors, a weight-p zero-sum with multiplicity p-1 each will exist, making the structure weakly zero-sum-free. Only by including the zero tail can you eliminate these weight-p zero-sums entirely and achieve strong zero-sum-freeness. I haven't formalized this yet, but it's a useful structural observation to keep in mind.
